Japan Looks Back, Warily, Seeking Way Forward
Japan Looks Back, Warily, Seeking Way Forward
By Jacob M. Schlesinger and Takashi Nakamichi
938 words
12 June 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TOKYO -- Japan's struggle to boost economic growth is being haunted by the ghost of a geisha-loving
finance minister who steered the country out of the Great Depression but was assassinated for trying to dial
back the stimulus.
Korekiyo Takahashi is one of the most divisive figures in the normally staid world of central banking. He gets
credit for pulling Japan out of the Depression ahead of other countries but is criticized for the soaring
inflation that occurred after he was killed at the hands of rebellious soldiers in 1936.
Nearly 80 years later, Japan once again is battling debilitating deflation, a cycle of falling prices and wages.
And Bank of Japan officials now face the difficult question of just how far they dare follow Mr. Takahashi, a
challenge complicated by the government's mammoth debt.
Specifically, just how aggressively will the central bank go beyond its core mission of monetary policy, or
price stability, and wade into fiscal policy: helping the government deal with its debt problem in a way that
minimizes spending cuts or tax increases that could threaten the tentative economic rebound? And can the
central bank do it without triggering hyperinflation or blowing up Japan's $8 trillion sovereign-bond market?
Debt levels in Asia have risen significantly in recent years, but Japan's government is by far the biggest
borrower in the region and second only to the U.S. government globally. Years of government deficits, weak
growth and deflation have pushed Japanese government debt to more than twice the country's annual
economic output, making it the most indebted country in the world by that measure.
This month, Prime Minister Shinzo Abe will announce a plan to stabilize the debt by 2020. To balance the
budget while maintaining economic growth and keeping the bond-ratings firms happy will require delicate
maneuvering. To do it without the help of the central bank will be nearly impossible.
That is where Mr. Takahashi's legacy looms.
A "bitter experience" was how then-Bank of Japan Gov. Masaaki Shirakawa described the Takahashi
program of central-bank support for government borrowing in a 2011 speech. Two years later, Haruhiko
Kuroda, the central-bank governor tapped by Mr. Abe to shred Mr. Shirakawa's cautious policies, said Mr.
Takahashi "achieved a significant result in overcoming the Showa Depression."
Mr. Takahashi's role in helping early 20th-century Japan catch up to the West made him a hero. He served as
Bank of Japan governor, briefly as prime minister and seven times as finance minister. He created Japan's
patent system and secured crucial foreign funds for Japan's shocking 1905 military victory over Russia.
While Mr. Takahashi won an elite peerage, he had humble roots and a raucous streak. The illegitimate son of
a court painter, he studied English with American missionaries, then moved to California as a teen, where he
was sold into indentured servitude and had to escape back to Japan. He once quit government for a
silver-mining venture in Peru, but returned home broke after discovering the mine already had been depleted.
A rare Japanese Christian, he also indulged openly a fondness for sake and geisha.
With global depression threatening Japan in 1931, Mr. Takahashi was summoned from retirement at age 77
to once again run the Finance Ministry. In a dispute foreshadowing today's divisions, Mr. Takahashi's
predecessor favored tight money, a strong yen and fiscal discipline, while Mr. Takahashi preferred powerful
stimulus.
He abandoned the gold standard, driving down the yen's value and lifting exports. He cut interest rates and
started deficit spending. But his most controversial act was to direct the Bank of Japan, then controlled by the
Finance Ministry, to buy newly issued bonds directly from the government.

--- Page 96 ---
Page 96 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
It is a line few central bankers dare to cross. Instead, central banks in modern Japan, the U.S. and Europe
have bought existing government bonds in the market, fearing they would be accused of printing money to
directly fund government deficits.
The effort helped pull Japan out of depression and deflation well before the U.S. and Europe. By 1935, Mr.
Takahashi decided to cut spending to prevent economic overheating. That made him a target of Japan's
increasingly aggressive military, which was demanding funds for its expanding China incursion. At 5 a.m. on
Feb. 26, 1936, two rebel officers plotting a coup stormed Mr. Takahashi's bedroom. One officer shot him with
a pistol and the other slashed him with a sword.
The attempted government takeover failed. But the military succeeded in cowing economic-policy makers.
After Mr. Takahashi's murder, inflation spiked to double-digit percentages.
In the decades since his death, policy makers blamed Mr. Takahashi, and not the military, for the ensuing
inflation. "This is the bank's biggest mistake in its 100-year history," the Bank of Japan wrote in its official
chronicle marking its 1982 centennial.
The Bank of Japan's view of Mr. Takahashi changed when Mr. Abe won a decisive election victory in 2012.
"My forerunner Takahashi has emboldened me," Mr. Abe said in a speech after taking office.
Two years into Takahashi-like boldness, "Abenomics" has yet to enjoy Takahashi-like success, with the
inflation rate stuck at zero, and possibly turning back negative this summer. One reason for the struggle: The
government's decision to focus on curbing debt as well as battling deflation. A modest 2014 sales-tax
increase pushed the economy into a recession from which it is just now escaping.

Page 97 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.

Business News: Wage Battle Looms for Gold Miners --- Talks expected to force South African
producers to swallow higher costs, scale back their output
By Alexandra Wexler
912 words
12 June 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
JOHANNESBURG -- South African gold producers, battling low prices and power outages, are gearing up for
a new struggle: wage talks that are expected to push up their costs and drag down production.
Unions representing more than 80% of the country's gold workers are demanding as much as double current
wages. Industry watchers predict the two unions will settle for much less, but producers such as Harmony
Gold Mining Co., which has been driven to cut costs by a yearslong slide in gold prices, could find it hard to
digest even a smaller bump in workers' pay. Wages make up about half of Harmony's costs.
"I don't think the country can afford to lose jobs, but we can't afford to continue to operate at a loss," said
Graham Briggs, the company's chief executive.
The National Union of Mineworkers said last month that Harmony was planning to cut more than 3,000 jobs
and idle one of its mines. Harmony said it is consulting with unions and other interested parties to explore a
possible restructuring of the money-losing mine.
For the first three months of the year, Harmony, whose shares have fallen 23% since the start of 2015,
posted a loss of $22 million on revenue of $292 million, down 11% from the same quarter a year earlier.
The aggressive wage demands reflect a violent power struggle between the established National Union of
Mineworkers and the upstart Association of Mineworkers and Construction Union, whose rivalry ignited a
wildcat strike in August 2012 that left dozens dead.
Last year, a wage battle between the unions and platinum producers led to a five-month strike, the country's
longest ever. Platinum production plummeted 15%, and South Africa's economic growth slipped below 2%.
The platinum industry is still struggling with the costly deal it made to end the strike, which raised miners'
starting wages as much as 60% over three years. Lonmin PLC, the world's No. 3 platinum producer, said last
month that it would reorganize its business and cut labor costs by 10%, or 3,500 jobs, risking another round
of strikes. Lonmin shares are down 22% since the beginning of the year.
The outlook is also grim for the gold sector. Gold is trading below $1,200 a troy ounce, down nearly 40%
from 2011 highs above $1,900 an ounce. The energy-intensive mining industry also has been pummeled by
frequent electrical outages, the result of disarray at South Africa's state-run power provider.
"The prospects of a long strike lasting over a month will be high," said Robert Besseling, principal analyst at
consulting firm IHS. He said AMCU's growing influence has raised the chances of a walkout because the
union's desire to draw members from NUM will push it to demand more from employers.
Some miners say they have left NUM because its leaders were too close to mine bosses and had lost touch
with their grievances.
"In the gold sector, we are still the majority, and we are recruiting vigorously," said NUM spokesman
Livhuwani Mammburu. "We're going to fight hard to make sure the lower categories of workers get big
increases."
AMCU didn't respond to requests for comment.
Gold Fields Ltd., the world's seventh-largest gold producer, sought to head off potential conflict by reaching
its own three-year deal in April to give its employees a 21% raise at its only South African mine, the
unprofitable South Deep, located an hour's drive southwest of Johannesburg.

--- Page 98 ---
Page 98 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Can you imagine us trying to fix all these problems and trying to negotiate an industrywide agreement that
could go on for months and months and months?" said Nick Holland, the company's CEO.
Gold Fields shares have fallen 24% since the start of 2015.
Gold Fields has been restructuring its business to cope with lower gold prices, in part by spinning off three of
its aging South African mines in 2013 to create Sibanye Gold Ltd. Gold Fields says it struck its own deal
because South Deep is South Africa's only fully mechanized gold mine, with a small, skilled workforce that
differentiates it from a conventional mine.
For the rest of the industry, IHS's Mr. Besseling predicts a compromise wage increase of around 10%, well
below what the unions are demanding but more than the inflation-matching wage increases of around 4% to
6% that gold companies have historically favored.
Last month, unions representing public-sector workers, including teachers, nurses and police officers,
secured a raise of 7%, or inflation plus 2.2 percentage points.
"We have started the dialogue with our workforce, explaining the challenges we've had in the South African
operation," said Srinivasan Venkatakrishnan, CEO of AngloGold Ashanti Ltd., the world's No. 3 gold
producer.
AngloGold beat its output projections in the quarter ended March 31, but a low gold price continues to weigh
on its results, he said. The company posted a loss of $1 million for the quarter.
Mr. Venkatakrishnan said he hopes to reach a deal that takes productivity into account, alongside job security
and job losses. "You've got to look at the long-term stability of the industry," he said. "Is it going to be an easy
process? I don't think so."

Outflows Hit Waddell & Reed Funds --- Two biggest units see $12.5 billion fly out door in sign of vulnerability to small investors
Outflows Hit Waddell & Reed Funds --- Two biggest units see $12.5 billion fly out door in sign of
vulnerability to small investors
By Matt Wirz
1,039 words
11 June 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Waddell & Reed Financial Inc., one of the biggest beneficiaries of the recent boom in mutual funds, has hit a
rough patch. Nervous investors pulled $12.5 billion out of the investment company's two largest mutual funds
over the past 12 months.
Waddell & Reed has grown primarily by marketing to mom-and-pop investors, who make up 86% of its
clientele. That makes it more susceptible to shifts in popular sentiment than larger competitors that also
invest for pension plans, insurers and sovereign-wealth funds.
The Overland, Kan.-based firm almost tripled in size from 2009 to 2013 -- it now manages about $123 billion
-- and pioneered a trend in "go-anywhere" mutual funds that, much like hedge funds, can trade almost
anything, from stocks to bonds to precious metals. Now, investment dollars are going the other way.
The go-anywhere fund took big losses from bets on gold and Asian casino stocks last year, rattling investors.
Separately, Waddell & Reed abruptly fired the manager of its junk-bond fund, triggering a wave of
redemptions. Combined outflows from the funds since April 2014 amount to about 10% of the firm's assets.
Waddell & Reed's outflows don't qualify as a run, in which investors worried about getting their money back
line up to redeem shares, creating a cash crunch that forces the firm to raise cash, sell assets or temporarily
halt redemptions.
Outflows have reached 12 consecutive months in Waddell & Reed's $24.8 billon go-anywhere Ivy Asset
Strategy Fund and 10 months in the $8 billion Ivy High Income Fund. A year earlier, the funds together held
$47.5 billion, according to Morningstar Inc. data.
"Both funds have experienced portfolio-management teams in place," a Waddell spokesman said in an email.
"Few funds in the industry experience the rapid growth that the Ivy Asset Strategy and High Income funds
have, and fewer still don't see growth slow or reverse at some point."
Wealth adviser Fieldpoint Private Advisors Inc. had more than $10 million invested in the junk-bond fund last
July, when Waddell & Reed announced the firing of the fund's manager, William Nelson. The firm said only
that Mr. Nelson was fired for cause unrelated to the fund's operation. Waddell declined to comment on the
firing, and Mr. Nelson couldn't be reached for comment.
"We removed the fund from our platform immediately," said Nick Markola, Fieldpoint's director of research,
who parked his clients' money in exchange-traded funds until it could be reinvested in actively managed
mutual funds. "You don't see managers terminated for cause often and in that situation we shoot first and aim
later."
The Asset Strategy Fund also experienced leadership changes last year when co-manager Ryan Caldwell left
the fund. Poor performance also contributed to the outflows, analysts say.
Fund manager Michael Avery has a reputation for aggressive trades, including the sale of $4.1 billion of
futures contracts credited in some published accounts with triggering the stock-market "flash crash" in May
2010. The Justice Department in April charged a U.K.-based trader with helping to cause that crash by
manipulating stock-index futures.
Mr. Avery's strategies attracted $12 billion of net inflows from 2009 to mid-2014.
But the fund lost 5% in March and April of 2014, according to Morningstar, when gold prices fell, then took
more losses on Asian casino stocks after a Chinese anticorruption campaign suppressed gambling in the
region.

--- Page 100 ---
Page 100 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Regulators are increasingly worried that a large asset manager will fall prey to a panic. The Securities and
Exchange Commission proposed in May more rigorous reporting requirements for investment firms to help
the regulator analyze the impact of market shocks and "identify funds that might be at risk . . . due to
increased redemptions," according to the proposal.
New SEC regulations allow money-market funds to use exit fees and "redemption gates" to slow or halt
outflows during a run but mutual-fund managers can't under the new regulations.
"This makes no sense to me at all," said Andrew Hofer, the head of bond research at wealth-management
firm Brown Brothers Harriman & Co., at a conference for bond-fund managers in Boston this month. "If gates
are now required features of money-market funds, why are regular [mutual] funds unable to gate?"
The SEC developed new rules for money-market funds in response to runs on such funds during the financial
crisis and is evaluating new guidelines to address risk in mutual funds, a person familiar with the regulations
said.
Mom-and-pop investors are notoriously fickle and can move in and out of funds quickly, said Michael Kim, an
analyst covering asset managers at Sandler O'Neill + Partners LP.
The herdlike movement of "retail" investors has more impact than ever before because mutual funds have
grown markedly since the financial crisis through a combination of inflows and investment returns.
The Ivy Asset Strategy Fund grew by about 30% to a record of $36 billion in the 12 months through February
2014.
"The fund is much bigger today, so the levels of outflows on an absolute basis are much bigger than in prior
cycles," Mr. Kim said.
Other small managers like Artisan Partners Distributors LLC and Virtus Investment Partners also suffered
significant outflows from franchise funds in recent months. A spokesman for Virtus declined to comment on
the outflows beyond the firm's disclosure in a May conference call that it had experienced net outflows of $2.2
billion in the first quarter related primarily to its Alpha Sector fund strategies. Artisan couldn't be reached for
comment.
Individual investors have flocked to alternative bond funds in recent years because low interest rates have
dragged down the yield of more traditional money-market and government-bond funds.
One concern is that when redemptions hit, bond funds can dump high-quality investments first because they
are more liquid, or easy to sell at high prices. The strategy can avoid immediate losses but leave remaining
shareholders holding a portfolio with fewer safe bonds and a higher proportion of risky ones.

The Middle Seat: What Gate Agents Really Think --- The pressure of last-minute decisions at Chicago's O'Hare International Airport; deciding...
The Middle Seat: What Gate Agents Really Think --- The pressure of last-minute decisions at
Chicago's O'Hare International Airport; deciding to bend the rules
By Scott McCartney
1,330 words
11 June 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Chicago -- They are the airline cattle wranglers.
Gate agents herd stressed passengers, corral luggage and listen to loud pleas all day long. They take the
brunt of the latest traveler troubles, including a dearth of free seat assignments for families. They are on front
lines of travel disruptions, which have been quite widespread so far this year.
Passengers are fed detailed information about seat maps, upgrade lists, standby lists and flight delays. But
more information means more demands for explanations.
"People say, 'I want this.' 'I want that.' 'Do the impossible for me.' It really is a tough job," says Suleman Tariq,
a United Airlines supervisor of gate agents at Chicago's O'Hare International Airport.
At two United flights at O'Hare recently, I got to see firsthand how the job has changed and where tensions
between travelers and airlines sometimes collide against a backdrop of minute-by-minute deadlines.
United agents have been under extra pressure for several years. The merger with Continental Airlines led to
an unfamiliar computer system that was plagued with problems. And for the past few years, United has
ranked among the lowest-performing airlines in key measures like delays and complaints, putting more angry
customers at the gate in front of agents.
But United agents say terminal life is improving. The computer system is running more smoothly and airport
gate areas are getting renovated. The airline is testing three design prototypes at O'Hare with new touches
like a computer screen on a swivel arm so the agent can show a skeptical passenger exactly where a
late-arriving plane got delayed or what seats are actually still available.
As gate agents set up for Flight 958, a 3:50 p.m. trip to London's Heathrow Airport on May 28, questions from
early-arriving passengers are almost always the same. On international flights, the most common question is
whether a meal will be served onboard. (Yes.)
Between welcome announcements and luggage-limit reminders, the puzzle-solving begins. Agents look for
ways to move passengers around, sometimes to a better seat with extra legroom, so that families can sit
together. They page customers and ask if they are traveling alone and willing to switch seats.
"We try to keep a few good seats in the back pocket," says Jasna Latic, one of the agents working Flight 958.
Sometimes agents have to decide which passengers will get on the flight and which will be left behind.
People making difficult international connections sometimes get priority.
The other common request at the gate is a free upgrade. Some customers still try charm, though airlines
have strict rules on how any free upgrades get processed. On international flights, even elite fliers have to
pay to upgrade in cash or miles or both.
There are exceptions. At United, if the coach cabin is overbooked on international trips, some lucky travelers
will get selected for empty business-class seats. Gate agents pick them by status and fare paid. If there is no
space crunch, business-class and first-class seats will either fly empty or be filled by employees or their
relatives. The airline doesn't want to just give away premium seats when they are empty.
Check-in cuts off for international flights one hour before departure, and as the clock ticks down on computer
screens, gate agent Alejandro Llerandi realizes two business-class passengers with United's highest status
level, Global Services, haven't yet checked in. Mr. Llerandi calls the Global Services check-in desk and asks
if they have seen the couple. (No.)

--- Page 103 ---
Page 103 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
He clicks an icon with a lock on it, closing Flight 958 to check-in at the airport lobby or online. But at 3 p.m.,
40 minutes before the door closes, the Global Services desk calls him back. They showed up.
"Do they have bags?" Mr. Llerandi asks. United wants at least an hour for baggage screening and loading.
Checked luggage on international flights has to be matched to passengers onboard.
They have only carry-on bags, so Mr. Llerandi decides to let them check in. He clicks the lock-out icon so the
couple can get boarding passes, then re-clicks to lock the flight. (Three coach passengers fail to check in.)
At boarding, gate agent Marie Danes looks closely to make sure passports have been signed to avoid fines
on airlines for transporting passengers with invalid passports. She catches four. Watching one passenger
scribble, she asks if he is a doctor.
"No, police chief," he snaps.
Mr. Llerandi and Ms. Latic start filling empty seats with vacationing employees and relatives. An employee
who started working for the company in 1988 is first on that list due to the length of her tenure. She and her
companion get two first-class seats.
Checking the passenger manifest, Ms. Danes finds seven people checked in but not boarded -- their boarding
passes weren't scanned. An agent goes on the plane to check each seat. All are onboard, mostly Global
Services customers who flashed their passports to gate agents and rushed on.
With manifests updated, she clicks a Final Boarding icon so airport displays change on Flight 958, then
Boarding Closed. Printouts of all 160 passengers are given to the captain and flight attendants. Mr. Llerandi
latches the door closed and Ms. Latic drives the jetbridge back. Ms. Danes declares it "a perfect flight."
Flight 1214 to New York's LaGuardia Airport is more of a challenge. Gate agents can predict conflict based
on destination. "Any flight going to New York -- it's a rough crowd," Mr. Tariq says. He and other agents add
that Las Vegas flights get a close eye for intoxicated passengers and flights to Beijing and other destinations
in China can be difficult to handle because customers like to board all at once.
About 40 minutes before the 5:05 p.m. departure, Flight 1214 has one seat open and three customers on the
standby list. Agents wait until 30 minutes before departure to close check-in on domestic flights and begin
filling empty seats.
The one open seat was 12F, a window seat in the Economy Plus extra-legroom section. Just before clearing
the standby list, a gold-level frequent flier with a middle seat, 23B, shows up at the counter seeking a trade.
He gets 12F.
Minutes later, Sarah Thrift, a management consultant booked on a later flight that already has been delayed,
runs up to the gate. She is No. 1 on the standby list -- after paying $75 to get there. On the phone, a United
representative who sold her the standby list had said an Economy Plus seat was available.
Gate agent Leonardo Gonzalez gives Ms. Thrift a boarding pass for the new lone available seat, 23B. She
protests strongly and Mr. Gonzalez apologetically offers to refund the $75 and put her back on her original,
delayed flight. She decides the bad seat is better than the delayed flight.
"The misinformation is really frustrating," Ms. Thrift says.
Boarding is almost complete and two customers are no-shows. One has an Economy Plus seat. Mr. Tariq,
the supervisor, goes onboard to confirm the seats are empty.
Ms. Thrift is switched to 10C, an aisle seat in Economy Plus. The two other customers on the standby list get
seats. But before they get their boarding passes, another woman from the later LaGuardia flight runs up
saying she wants to get on Flight 1214. Too late, Mr. Gonzalez says. When he pages the two standby
passengers to give them seats, the woman grows angry.
"Don't you go by status? You're still calling people. Are you going to take my name or not?"" she yells at Mr.
Gonzalez. She stomps off, shouting, "It's the same mess every week."

Dow Transports Near a Correction
By Corrie Driebusch and Stephanie Yang

U.S. stocks were little changed, with the Dow Jones Transportation Average falling closer toward correction
territory.
Airline shares tumbled, extending Monday's losses, putting the 20-stock index that tracks some of the largest
U.S. airlines, railroads and trucking companies within striking distance of a "correction," or a decline of 10%
from a recent high.
In the past two sessions, the Dow Jones Transportation Average has fallen 2.4%, and it is off 9.9% since it
last hit an all-time high on Dec. 29, 2014.
The fall in transportation stocks is commonly considered a warning signal for the broader stock market,
traders say.
The airline industry was the worst-performing industry in the S&P 500 on Tuesday, with Southwest Airlines
down $1.52, or 4.2%, to $34.59. For the first two days of this week, Southwest Airlines is off 6.9%.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average shed 2.51 points, or 0.01%, to 17764.04. The S&P 500 gained 0.87 point,
or 0.04%, to 2080.15, while the Nasdaq Composite fell 7.76 points, or 0.2%, to 5013.87.
Overseas early Wednesday, Japan's Nikkei was up 0.4% and Australia's S&PASX 200 was down 0.3%. The
Shanghai Composite was down 0.4% after MSCI Inc. said it expects to add China's A-shares, into its

While stock volumes were slightly higher than on Monday in the U.S., they remained fairly muted. On
Tuesday, 5.85 billion shares changed hands, compared with the year-to-date average of 6.5 billion shares a
day.
"It's been a bit of a snoozer," said Joe Saluzzi, partner at Themis Trading, adding he believes trading will
remain subdued until the Federal Reserve raises interest rates.
Many investors expect the Fed's first rate increase since 2006 to happen later this year, possibly as early as
the central bank's September meeting.
Although trading volumes have remained low in recent sessions, traders said they expect this to change in
the final weeks of June. Later this month, a host of options and futures contracts are set to expire, which can
create a flurry of trading activity. Also, the Russell indexes are scheduled for a rebalancing, which typically
raises trading volumes in small-company shares.
While stocks have pulled back in recent sessions, with the Dow ending in negative territory for the year on
Monday, they remain stuck in a range.
"There are full valuations in the equity market," said Mark Luschini, chief investment strategist at Janney
Montgomery Scott. "Full valuations demand perfect news, or at least better news than what we've been
getting."
The S&P 500 is trading at roughly 18 times the past 12 months of earnings. That is higher than its 10-year
average of 15.8, according to FactSet.
Despite this lack of urgency to scoop up shares of companies, many investors believe stocks will continue to
trend higher if for no other reason than a lack of other viable options.

Investing in Funds & ETFs: A Monthly Analysis --- Spotlight / Vanguard Total Bond Market Index: Buy

By Kirsten Grind

Vanguard Total Bond Market Index fund (VBMFX) has captured one of the most sought-after titles in money
management: the world's largest bond mutual fund.
No. 1 had been Pacific Investment Management Co.'s Pimco Total Return fund (PTTAX), whose asset size
dropped from a peak of $293 billion in April 2013 under star manager Bill Gross, who left last year, to $110.4
billion in April, the latest data available. That is when the Vanguard fund, run by Joshua Barrickman, took the
lead. The fund's assets rose to $117.3 billion in April, not including an ETF share class that fund researcher
Morningstar counts separately.
While Morningstar recommends Vanguard Total Bond, it gives it only a silver rating, not gold, because of its
sensitivity to a faster-than-expected rise in interest rates. It is heavy on low-yielding government bonds. Still,
analyst Thomas Boccellari, in a report last year, called it "an excellent core fixed-income holding" -- with low
fees that "give this fund a sustainable edge."
Mr. Boccellari couldn't be reached for comment, but another Morningstar analyst, John Gabriel, says the
fund's rating stands. "While it will track the benchmark as advertised, it's still important to understand what its
investment exposures are," he says.
John Woerth, a Vanguard spokesman, says, "Investors hold bonds for ballast. The fund's weighting in
governments will offer greater downside protection in the event of an equity-market drop."

Stocks Cap a Volatile Week With a Decline --- Solid jobs report offers evidence economy is pulling out
of slump, sparks selloff in bonds
By Dan Strumpf
1,002 words
6 June 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stocks fell as a strong May jobs report cemented expectations that the Federal Reserve will begin raising
borrowing costs this fall. Stocks capped a volatile few days that left major benchmarks slightly lower for the
week.
The solid jobs report sparked a deeper selloff in the bond market, boosting the yield on the benchmark
10-year Treasury note to its highest level in eight months.
Traders and investors said the jobs report offered evidence that the U.S. economy was pulling itself out of its
first-quarter slump. While a firming economy is usually a good backdrop for stocks, investors say the hiring
pickup keeps the Fed on track to start raising short-term interest rates as soon as September, potentially
boosting borrowing costs for businesses and consumers.
Stocks spent the Friday session swinging between gains and losses before settling with a modest decline.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average lost 56.12 points, or 0.3%, to 17849.46, bouncing back from an loss of as
much as 83 points earlier in the session. The S&P 500 index fell 3.01, or 0.1%, to 2092.83. The Nasdaq
Composite Index, meanwhile, rose 9.33, or 0.2%, to 5068.46.
Stocks ended the week lower, after an early-week rally petered out. The S&P 500 fell 0.7% for the week,
while the Dow shed 0.9%.
Major stock benchmarks have pulled back in recent sessions from records reached just last month, weighed
down by a cocktail of uneven economic data, murkiness over the Fed's course of action and lofty valuations.
The Dow is off 2.5% from its record set May 19. The S&P 500 is down 1.8% from its May 21 high.
"For the economy, the bottom line is that [the jobs data] is a good number," said Brent Schutte, senior
investment strategist at BMO Global Asset Management, which oversees $249 billion. But he added: "To the
extent that it brings people closer to believing that the Fed will raise rates in September, it will be a near-term
hit to the equity markets and the bond markets."
Bond prices fell sharply, lifting the yield on the 10-year Treasury note to an eight-month high of 2.402%, from
2.309% on Thursday. That is the highest closing level since Oct. 6. Bond prices fall as their yields rise.
For the week, the 10-year Treasury yield climbed 0.305 percentage point, the biggest weekly rise since June
2013, when bonds were rattled by the "taper tantrum," or fears over reduced bond buying from the Fed.
The bond market has sold off since late April after a strong run-up in price over the past year. Many investors
are recasting their portfolios as they believe the rise in bond yields reflects an improving economic and
inflation outlook in the U.S. and the eurozone.
"The rise in bond yields is a natural process, and it is a sign that the U.S. economy is moving in the right
direction," said Poul Kristensen, a portfolio manager who helps oversee $9.84 billion at New York Life
Investment Management's Strategic Asset Allocation & Solutions Group.
Mr. Kristensen said he has been positioned for a rise in U.S. interest rates by curbing his holdings of
Treasury bonds and U.S. stocks. He prefers stocks in Japan and Europe.
The Labor Department said 280,000 jobs were added in May and the unemployment rate ticked up slightly to
5.5%.
Investors have been closely tracking the monthly jobs report for clues on the pace of economic growth and
the course of the Fed's interest-rate increases. Several economic indicators have shown that the U.S.

--- Page 108 ---
Page 108 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
economy and corporate-profit growth stumbled in the first quarter. On Thursday, the International Monetary
Fund cut its forecasts for U.S. growth and called for the Fed to hold off on its first rate increase until 2016.
Also Thursday, Fed governor Daniel Tarullo said the economy appears to have "lost some momentum."
But other indicators have been more positive, including strong May auto sales and improving trade data in
April. Most Fed officials have said they expect to begin raising the central bank's benchmark short-term
interest rate from zero later this year, and investors view a September rate increase as now the most likely
outcome.
"It is a solid report and certainly puts the Fed on course to raise rates in September," said Christopher
Sullivan, who oversees $2.4 billion as chief investment officer at the United Nations Federal Credit Union in
New York. "If growth continues to improve and wage inflation ticks higher," bond yields have room to rise, he
said.
Utility stocks and other higher-yielding corners of the stock market sustained the biggest losses Friday. Such
higher-yielding stocks compete most directly with bonds for investor assets. Utilities stocks in the S&P 500 fell
1.3%.
Financial stocks gained on expectations that higher rates will boost margins. J.P. Morgan Chase posted the
biggest gain in the Dow, rising $1.09, or 1.6%, to $67.42. Shares of Goldman Sachs Group rose 1.58, or
0.8%, to 210.45. The S&P 500 Financials index added 0.6%.
Also pulling ahead were shares of small-cap companies, which tend to be more U.S.-focused and thus
perform better in a strengthening economy. The Russell 2000 small-cap index gained 9.72, or 0.8%, to
1261.01.

European stocks fell, with the Stoxx Europe 600 shedding 0.9%. The index fell 2.7% for the week.

Gold Gains Allure as Economy Trips
Commodities
Gold Gains Allure as Economy Trips
By Tatyana Shumsky
1,076 words
2 June 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Some investors who aren't sold on the strength of the U.S. economic recovery are taking a shine to gold.
After shunning the precious metal for years in favor of bonds and stocks, which often pay a steady income,
investors are returning to the gold market to safeguard their wealth. Lofty valuations in stocks and bonds,
which have rallied in recent years while gold prices slumped, also are prompting some investors to revisit
gold amid fears of a downdraft in these markets.
Some traders also are pointing to easy money policies in Europe, which have pushed deflation worries off the
table, and to similar efforts by other global central banks that could ultimately reignite inflation. These traders
are buying gold in the hope it will keep its value better than other assets if consumer prices surge or if
currencies lose their purchasing power.
In a sign that some investors are wading back into the market, the number of net bullish bets on gold
reached an 11-week high on May 19, slipping slightly the week ended May 26, according to the latest data
from the U.S. Commodity Futures Trading Commission.
The likelihood of higher interest rates in the U.S. has been a concern for investors in gold, which doesn't
pay interest or dividends and costs money to hold. But gold buyers say those worries are receding as U.S.
economic growth continues to disappoint. This has led investors to shift their expectations away from rapidly
tighter U.S. monetary policy toward only modest rate increases. Gold should remain competitive with
interest-bearing assets in this environment, and could rally if inflation pressures resurface, these traders say.
"Gold is still cheap relative to fixed income [assets]. . .gold could really pop and move," said Nicholas
Johnson, who helps manage $20 billion invested in commodities at Pacific Investment Management Co.
Investors buying gold aren't expecting another global recession. Instead, they say that years in which gold
was passed over for other assets has pushed the metal's price too low, and now it is ripe for a rebound.
Gold prices tumbled 29% in 2013 and 2014, a selloff driven largely by the Federal Reserve's exit from its
post-financial-crisis stimulus measures and expectations for a rise in interest rates.
Although gold prices inched higher in May, they remain close to 4 1/2-year lows. On Monday, gold futures
for August delivery, the most actively traded contract, fell $1.10, or 0.1%, to $1,188.70 a troy ounce on the
Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Gold prices already reflect the likelihood of a rate increase -- or two -- in coming months, say investors who
are skeptical that global growth will pick up enough to warrant steep tightening of U.S. monetary policy.
Meanwhile, bond yields world-wide are at historically low levels. In Europe, some safer types of debt sport
negative yields -- meaning that investors are paying to lend money. Bond yields fall as prices rise.
Concerns about soaring valuations of bonds -- as well as stocks and other assets fueled by years of cheap
money -- leave those markets vulnerable to big swings that can erode returns, investors say. That is
bolstering the appeal of gold, which tends to retain its value in times of turmoil, they say.
Matthias Kuzinski, who oversees $150 million of commodities investments at Lupus Alpha Asset
Management in Frankfurt, put bullish wagers on gold last month amid a sharp selloff in German government
debt that rattled financial markets.
Gold hit a three-month high amid the rout in European bonds in mid-May but has since pared those gains.

--- Page 111 ---
Page 111 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Kuzinski expects the U.S. economy's softness during the first quarter to persist. While recent economic
reports have improved, they don't illustrate the robust growth that would spur aggressive tightening of
monetary policy, Mr. Kuzinski said.
"Waiting for a strong pickup in the U.S. with the whole world struggling is a bit too enthusiastic," Mr. Kuzinski
said, adding that he doesn't expect higher rates this year. "Even if we got a small hike in the U.S., I can't
believe it will follow with more. A slow rate-hike cycle wouldn't hurt gold in the long run."
Money managers' bets on higher gold prices in the week ended May 26 exceeded bets that gold will fall by
73,025 Comex futures and options contracts, according to the CFTC. This is down slightly from 86,672
contracts a week earlier, the widest gap since the start of March.
To be sure, many investors aren't as upbeat on gold, citing potential headwinds from a rising dollar. Gold is
priced in dollars and as the greenback strengthens, gold becomes more expensive for investors who use
other currencies to fund their purchases. The WSJ Dollar Index, which tracks the U.S. currency's value
against 16 others, has risen 5.7% this year.
"Our outlook is for higher rates and for the dollar to go higher, so it's the worst time to be in gold," said
Sameer Samana, global strategist with Wells Fargo Advisors, which oversees $1.4 trillion in assets.
Still, some market experts expect the dollar's rally to be tripped up by weak economic indicators. U.S. growth
contracted 0.7% in the first three months of the year.
"The economy will have quite a hole to climb out of," said Edward Meir, senior commodities strategist with
brokerage INTL FCStone. "We think the Fed will raise rates once and then they're not going to do anything
for a while and that will be good for gold."
Other investors topping up their gold holdings cite concerns about unsustainable government debt levels
after years of asset buying by central banks seeking to pump money into economies. Gold buyers predict this
will boost inflation, which erodes the value of bonds and cash.
"Gold as a flight-to-quality asset holds as much validity for us today, as it did in 2005 and 2006, when we first
started buying it," said Michael Tiedemann, who manages $9.5 billion as chief investment officer at
Tiedemann Wealth Management.
Mr. Tiedemann recently purchased gold with profits made in stocks to maintain a 3% allocation in his
portfolio. "We wouldn't hold it if we didn't think it would appreciate in value," he said.

Easy Access to Money Keeps U.S. Oil Pumping --- Cost cuts, heavy inflow of capital help industry defy predictions of bust after crude prices...
Easy Access to Money Keeps U.S. Oil Pumping --- Cost cuts, heavy inflow of capital help industry
defy predictions of bust after crude prices slide
By Russell Gold
1,094 words
1 June 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Wall Street's generous supply of funds to U.S. oil drillers helped create the American energy boom. Now that
same access to easy money is keeping them going, despite oil prices that are languishing around $60 a
barrel.
The flow of money into oil has allowed U.S. companies to avoid liquidity problems and kept American crude
production from falling sharply.
Even though more than half of the rigs that were drilling new wells in September have been banished to
storage yards, in mid-May nearly 9.6 million barrels of oil a day were pumped across the country, the highest
level since 1970, according to the most recent federal data.
Helped by a ready supply of money, the flow of oil from the U.S. could keep crude prices low for the
remainder of 2015 and beyond.
It wasn't supposed to happen this way. As crude prices began to plunge last year, many energy experts
predicted a repeat of 1986, when U.S. oil companies lost their funding and the industry collapsed into a
yearslong bust. Without money, companies had to slow or even stop drilling for the crude that helped create a
global glut. Many were forced to sell out to rivals or go bankrupt.
But the gloomy scenario of that downturn hasn't played out on a large scale this time. That is because banks,
private-equity firms and institutional investors have continued to pour money into the sector even as oil
companies slashed billions of dollars in spending from their budgets and laid off more than 100,000 workers.
"What makes this downturn different is there is a lot more capital available," says Pearce Hammond, a
managing director at Houston-based energy investment bank Simmons & Co. International.
Interest rates remain at historic lows, which creates significant cash in search of good investments. That
makes drilling attractive to some investors even though crude prices are $50 a barrel lower than they were a
year ago. Historically, energy companies have offered competitive returns.
On top of that, energy shares are lagging behind the overall stock market. Over the past year, the S&P 500 is
up 9.6%, but energy stocks in the index are down 17.6%. Some investors, betting on an oil price rebound,
regard energy stocks as a relative bargain.
Private-equity firms are on pace to invest a record $20.6 billion in startup oil and gas companies this year,
according to Preqin, which tracks private-equity expenditures.
That is making oilmen like Lance Langford very popular. A veteran of Statoil ASA's operations in North
Dakota, Mr. Langford and several colleagues recently formed Luxe Energy LLC and secured $500 million
from Natural Gas Partners, a Dallas-based private-equity firm. Luxe is using the money to acquire oil and gas
leases in West Texas.
"Essentially, everyone that we talked to except for one small firm, wanted to do a deal with us," says Mr.
Langford, chief executive of Luxe. "There is a lot of money out there."
Many energy-market experts say that what the industry needs right now is discipline, not more drilling
investment.
Last fall, Saudi Arabia refused to cut its oil production to keep crude prices from falling, leaving a gaggle of
American oil producers and their financial backers as the principal mechanism for putting a floor under oil
prices.

--- Page 113 ---
Page 113 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries is set to meet again this week in Vienna, but is
expected to shirk off pressure to cut production and keep pumping all it can.
"I've been surprised by the amount of money that has come into the sector" since oil prices started falling,
said Paul Korus, chief financial officer of Cimarex Energy Co., a midsize energy company based in Denver.
Much of that stems from a belief that investors should buy now when oil prices are low, he says. "We all think
there will be some sort of recovery, but we don't know when it will recover and what it will recover to."
Cimarex is one of several outfits to tap public markets by issuing more shares. In the first three months of
2015, U.S.-listed companies issued $16.7 billion of new shares and convertible bonds, the highest level since
2010, according to Dealogic.
Most are using the cash to clean up their balance sheets. But Cimarex, which raised nearly $750 million in
late May, plans to spend it to drill more wells in Texas, Oklahoma and New Mexico. The company had cut its
rig count to six from 20; plans now call for nearly 20 rigs to be drilling again by the end of the year.
Since U.S. energy companies feasted on cheap capital during the boom, and financial markets didn't mete
out capital discipline analysts say other companies may follow Cimarex's lead and resume drilling.
"A lot of the industry seems to function that if you give them a dollar, they will spend it. That leaves the capital
markets as the only source of discipline," said Michael Levi, a senior fellow at the Council on Foreign
Relations. "If there is more capital than is warranted, you are going to see companies pick up drilling in the
second half of the year and that will put pressure on prices."
Banks aren't reining in oil-company borrowing -- at least not yet. Loan officers surveyed by the Federal
Reserve in April said they expect an increase in energy companies unable to pay back their loans, and were
preparing by restructuring agreements.
Many companies have other options. Goodrich Petroleum Corp., concerned that its line of credit would be
reduced, obtained a $100 million "second-lien" debt deal at a relatively high 8% interest rate.
Companies that aim to sell oil and gas fields to stay solvent will find buyers. When SM Energy Co. offered up
properties in Louisiana, Oklahoma and Texas, most bidders were private-equity-backed companies, said
people familiar with the deal.
Of course, not every company has secured the funds its needs to survive. This spring Halcon Resources
Corp., a Houston-based oil company, swapped some of its debt for shares, a move that Standard & Poor's
classified as a selective default.
S&P analyst Carin Dehne-Kiley said it won't be the last. "There will be more defaults before the year is up,"
she said.

Stocks Droop, Closing Out a Weak Month --- Worries about Greece, weak economic data weighed on shares; Dow rose 1% in May
Friday's Markets
Stocks Droop, Closing Out a Weak Month --- Worries about Greece, weak economic data weighed on
shares; Dow rose 1% in May
By Corrie Driebusch
740 words
30 May 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks slumped, concluding a sluggish trading month that featured a handful of record closes amid
uncertainty over the economy's health and the likely timetable for expected Federal Reserve interest-rate
increases.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average declined 115.44 points, or 0.6%, to 18010.68, after earlier falling as much
as 158 points. Shares were weighed down by fresh worries about Greece, which has yet to secure a
financing deal with its international creditors, and data that showed continued weakness in U.S. growth.
Friday's trading volume was the highest in a month on the New York Stock Exchange, though May was the
slowest month in U.S. trading in 2015. Trading volumes surged toward the end of the session, which is
common on the last day of the month as investors rebalance their stock positions.
Despite Friday's declines, the Dow industrials and the S&P 500 both gained 1% during a month that featured
a raft of seemingly conflicting economic indicators. The Dow set two records in May and the S&P set four.
Some data, such as an apparent resurgence in home building, seemed to suggest the U.S. economy is
indeed improving. Others, such as soft consumer-spending readings and slackening jobs growth, cast some
doubt on an economic bounce back. Investors are watching for signals on the timing of the Fed's plan to raise
interest rates, an event that some investors worry may hurt stock performance.
"The market is in a wait-and-see mode to see: Was the weakness in the first quarter temporary or was it
something more permanent," said Anwiti Bahuguna, senior portfolio manager at Columbia Threadneedle
Investments, which manages roughly $500 billion.
May was marked not only by slow trading but also by extremely low volatility. During the month only about 6.1
billion shares were changing hands a day, making it the slowest month in trading since November. The
market's "fear gauge," the CBOE Volatility Index, languished near low levels seen before the financial crisis.
On Friday, the S&P 500 lost 13.40 points, or 0.6%, to 2107.39, and the Nasdaq Composite shed 27.95
points, or 0.5%, to 5070.03.
New data Friday confirmed that the U.S. economy hit a slow patch in the first quarter and called into question
the prevailing narrative that the slowdown was just a blip.
On Friday, the Commerce Department said in its second reading of U.S. economic output for the first quarter
that gross domestic product shrank at a 0.7% rate. The initial reading of first-quarter GDP showed the U.S.
economy slowed sharply, growing at just 0.2%. Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal had
expected that to be revised downward to a 1% contraction in Friday's reading.
Also weighing on stocks Friday morning was a decline in the Chicago Business Barometer, known as the
Chicago PMI, a survey of Chicago-area purchasing managers that provides insight into companies' business
plans.
The decline, which put the index below 50, indicates that factory activity is contracting.
Separately Friday, a reading on consumer sentiment showed U.S. consumer optimism in May was higher
than expected but still down sharply from the end-of-April reading.
U.S. stocks also were hurt by news out of Europe.

--- Page 116 ---
Page 116 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
International Monetary Fund Managing Director Christine Lagarde said in a German newspaper interview that
a Greek exit from the euro is a possibility, contradicting comments from European Central Bank officials. The
Greek government, meanwhile, said Thursday that it aims to have a deal in place to secure fresh financing by
Sunday.
Jim Dunigan, chief investment officer at PNC Wealth Management, said he believes the country will come to
an agreement with its creditors. But, he said, "Greece's problems are not going to go away overnight."
Germany's DAX fell 2.3%, and France's CAC-40 dropped 2.5%.
In other markets, gold futures rose 0.1% to $1,189.40 an ounce.
In corporate news, Humana shares jumped $36.24, or 20%, to $214.65 after The Wall Street Journal reported
that the health insurer is considering selling the company and that it has received indications of takeover
interest.

The Capital Behind Those Public Pensions
Letters to the Editor
The Capital Behind Those Public Pensions
148 words
30 May 2015

The Dow Jones Industrial Average slumped 1%, its largest decline in nearly a month, as a fresh batch of U.S.

the Dow and the S&P 500 set records last week.
But Tuesday, a mixed reading on demand for big-ticket items in the U.S. and renewed uncertainty over
Greece's coming debt payments were enough to prompt a rout, traders said. Several market participants

uneven economic growth and anemic growth in corporate profits.

Cantor Fitzgerald.

500 index lost 21.86 points, or 1%, to 2104.20, while the Nasdaq Composite Index fell 56.61 points, or 1.1%,
to 5032.75 Both indexes sustained their biggest loss since May 5.

200 was down 0.7%, Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index was down 0.6% and Japan's Nikkei was off 0.1%.

currently trades at 18.4 times the past 12 months of earnings, according to FactSet, the highest in five years
and up from 16.4 a year ago. The 10-year average trailing P/E is 15.8.
And while investors' worst fears about the first-quarter earnings season were avoided, money managers are
far from sanguine about the outlook for corporate profits for the remainder of this year.
Profits for S&P 500 companies are on track to grow 0.3% in the first quarter, with 488 companies reporting,
according to FactSet. Though it is an improvement over the earnings decline of nearly 5% forecast at the start
of the earnings season, it is still the weakest pace of corporate-profit growth since 2012.
In the meantime, analysts already are calling for an earnings contraction of 4.6% in the second quarter. They
don't expect profits to begin growing again until the fourth quarter.
The pullback follows a raft of economic data Tuesday, including an early report showing demand for big-ticket
manufactured goods in the U.S. in April fell 0.5% from a month earlier. Economists had expected a more
modest decline of 0.1%, though business investment showed signs of picking up.
Also, home prices continued to climb in March, but less than expected, while new-home sales and consumer
confidence rose. The week ahead will bring an updated reading on first-quarter gross domestic product
expected to show a 1% decline.

Currencies: Dollar Trounces Yen, Euro, Canada's Loonie --- U.S. economic data help greenback soar
to nearly eight-year high against Japan currency
By James Ramage
759 words
27 May 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The dollar rallied broadly on Tuesday, driven by a recent flurry of economic data in the U.S. that has
bolstered some investors' confidence in the growth outlook.
The greenback soared to a nearly eight-year high against the Japanese yen, a one-month high against the
euro and a six-week high against the Canadian dollar. The buck also rallied against many emerging-market
currencies, hitting its highest level against the Brazilian real in nearly two months and against the Indonesian
rupiah since March 10.
The dollar's strength carried over from Friday, when inflation data for April underscored a pickup in growth
and Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen said the central bank was on track to raise short-term
benchmark interest rates this year. Most U.S. financial markets were closed Monday for the Memorial Day
holiday, and currency trading was subdued.
On Tuesday, the release of data showing gains in business investment, the housing market and consumer
confidence added fuel to the dollar's rally. Data from earlier in the year also were revised higher.
"As you get better data, you're getting more people thinking we'll see a recovery, which is good for the dollar,"
said Charles St-Arnaud, economist and currencies strategist for Nomura Securities.
Tuesday's sharp appreciation in the dollar is the latest big swing to ruffle foreign-exchange markets. An
eight-month dollar rally sputtered in March when the Fed lowered its forecast for growth, inflation and
benchmark interest rates in the coming years after the U.S. economy hit a soft patch. That sowed doubts
among investors who were betting the Fed would strike a tightening stance while other central banks pursued
easing policies. Higher rates make a currency more attractive for investors.
The dollar's rise sent ripples through commodity markets. Prices of crude oil, gold, copper and grains
tumbled Tuesday, pushing the S&P GSCI commodity-trading index down 2.1% to a four-week low.
The greenback rallied to its biggest one-day gain against the yen since Feb. 6, jumping to 123.11 yen, the
highest level since July 2007.
Against the euro, the dollar appreciated 1%, climbing to a one-month high of $1.0874. Some traders said
worries that Greece would default on its debt were exerting downward pressure on the common currency.
The dollar's strength came against a selloff in U.S. stocks. Some market watchers said that reflected
concerns about higher rates.
"The data spooked investors a little bit," said James Hosker, director of equities strategy in the Americas at
Societe Generale. "Equities investors don't want to see interest rates climbing too quickly."
Recent gains in the buck are unlikely to propel the dollar to the kinds of gains it saw in the second half of
2014 and the start of 2015, said Axel Merk, chief investment officer of Merk Investments, which has about
$400 million under management. Traders still are trying to gauge whether the U.S. economy is on sound
footing, so new data points bring on renewed volatility, Mr. Merk said.
"I'm not buying that we have this resurgence right now," he said. "I'm not sure the rally has too [much
support]. Whenever the dollar surges, people jump on that story. And as the dollar weakens, people jump on
that story, too."
"It won't be a clear shot upward here," Mr. Merk added. "I expect some back and forth in the dollar's move."

strengthen declined 10% to $26 billion in the week that ended May 19 from the previous week.

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The New Basics: Think Clearly in a Bull Market
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The New Basics: Think Clearly in a Bull Market
By Jonathan Clements
884 words
23 May 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
After six years of rising U.S. stock prices, investors are no doubt richer. But they may be thinking a little less
clearly.
"In a bull market, there's a tendency for investors to think they're brilliant," says Brad Barber, a finance
professor at the University of California, Davis, and an expert in behavioral finance. Indeed, as share prices
climb, investors' confidence grows and they start making all kinds of dubious claims.
Here are seven comments you have probably heard from friends -- and that may have escaped your own lips.
"I've beaten the market."
Investors often compare their global stock portfolio to the S&P 500, an index of U.S. large-company stocks.
That's an easy comparison in a year like 2015, when stocks in foreign countries and shares of smaller U.S.
firms are outpacing the S&P 500.
The better performance of foreign and small stocks can boost a portfolio's return, so it beats the S&P 500 --
even if the portfolio isn't beating an index that tracks the global stock market.
If the S&P 500 doesn't provide a favorable comparison, investors will often change indexes or ignore
one-year results and instead focus on three-year or five-year returns.
Or they might not use an index at all -- and instead compare their results to the mediocre actively managed
mutual funds their brother-in-law bought.
"My stock picks have made so much money."
Share prices have tripled since 2009, so anybody who owned stocks over the past six years should have
notched healthy gains. "But we ascribe the gains to our own skill," says Mr. Barber.
This has an upside: Self-confident individuals are often happier. Problem is, they also tend to trade too much
and make big, undiversified investment bets.
That tendency can get worse as stocks climb higher, according to Mr. Barber. Investors not only grow more
cocky as their portfolios grow fatter, but also they feel as if they're ahead of the game. Like casino gamblers
who are lucky early in the evening, "you get this 'house money' effect," he explains. "You're willing to take on
more risk, because you feel like you're playing with gains."
"It's the Fed's fault."
What happens when our investments don't pan out? Instead of blaming ourselves, we blame others.
"When our bond portfolios have done well in recent years, it's because we made good decisions," says John
Nofsinger, author of "The Psychology of Investing" and a finance professor at the University of Alaska
Anchorage. "But when it comes to the bond market's recent bad performance, we blame the Fed."
Bonds have struggled over the past month, as the yield on the benchmark 10-year Treasury note has climbed
from below 1.9% to above 2.2%, driving down bond prices.
Alternatively, we might simply rewrite history. "We misremember to make ourselves feel better," Mr. Nofsinger
says. "We just ignore the fact that our 401(k) didn't do that well last year."
"My portfolio has grown so much."

--- Page 130 ---
Page 130 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
That may be true. But how much of the growth has come from the savings we have added, rather than from
investment gains?
If we're disappointed by our investment performance, "we can change from measuring investment return to
investment level," Mr. Nofsinger says. "If my return is negative but contributions allowed the level to still
increase, I can focus on the level."
"It's only a paper loss."
If we buy a $10 stock that falls to $8, we have lost money. But many investors don't see it that way.
"People feel that paper losses are different from real losses," notes Meir Statman, a finance professor at
California's Santa Clara University and author of "What Investors Really Want." The reason: There's still a
chance that the shares will bounce back.
What happens when we finally sell that losing stock? We quietly drop it from our mental arithmetic -- and now
we can boast to our neighbors that we've made money on every stock we own.
"I bought it for diversification."
When an investment doesn't perform the way we expect, we will often mentally reclassify it. Take gold, which
was all the rage after its price jumped more than sevenfold over the decade through 2011.
But instead of continuing to soar, gold has lost more than a third of its value since September 2011. What to
do? We might drop it from the "it will make me rich" portion of our portfolio -- and reclassify it as "it might do
well if we get another financial crisis."
"This time, I'll get out before the crash."
In hindsight, market tops and bottoms seem obvious, so we figure we'll see the next one coming. But the
current bull market's peak will probably be just as murky as the low point of the last bear market, in March
2009.
"We can now see that that was the bottom of the market," Mr. Statman says. "But at the time, you couldn't
see the pattern."
---
Jonathan Clements is the author of the "Jonathan Clements Money Guide 2015."
ClementsMoney@gmail.com

Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The S&P 500 ground out a fresh all-time high, as stocks gained broadly in quiet trading.
The S&P index added 4.97 points, or 0.2%, to 2130.82, surpassing its most recent record, which was
reached on Monday.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average gained 0.34 point to 18285.74, only 0.1% shy of its Tuesday record of

The gains in the broad market came on the back of quiet trading in stocks, as major benchmarks have
hugged a narrow range all week.
With the first-quarter earnings season largely complete, many investors say they are sitting tight as they await
new market-moving catalysts. Trading volumes have fallen sharply in recent sessions ahead of the Memorial
Day weekend.
"It does seem like the path of least resistance is likely higher," said Justin Wiggs, a trader at Stifel Nicolaus.
"Volumes have been atrocious of late. I don't think that's going to change anytime soon with the holiday
week."
This week is on pace to be the slowest week in stock trading since the week including New Year's Day. Just
5.6 billion shares traded hands on Thursday, marking the sixth-slowest trading day in 2015.
Daily volume this year has been averaging about 6.6 billion shares.
Adding to the quiet is a belief that any interest-rate increase by the Federal Reserve remains off on the
horizon following a significant economic slowdown in the first quarter.
Many investors think the Fed won't raise rates until the end of 2015.
"They need to see inflation, they need to see job growth, and they need to see wage growth," said Brad
McMillan, chief investment officer for Commonwealth Financial Network, which manages about $97 billion.
Signs of sustained wage growth are especially important to the Fed, he said, because it could spur consumer
spending and drive economic growth.

prices rise.

Commodities Rally Hit by Stronger Dollar --- U.S. currency's reversal has battered markets, including crude oil, copper, gold and corn
Commodities Rally Hit by Stronger Dollar --- U.S. currency's reversal has battered markets, including
crude oil, copper, gold and corn
By Ira Iosebashvili
660 words
20 May 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Commodity prices tumbled, as a resurgent dollar and concerns about weakness in China's economy pushed
investors out of everything from crude oil to copper and corn.
The S&P GSCI index, which tracks prices of 24 commodities, recorded its biggest one-day drop in more than
a month and ended at its lowest since April 28.
Before that, commodities markets, led by a recent rebound in crude oil, staged a surprising two-month rally
that had pushed the index to its highs for the year. Driving the gains was a faltering greenback, which many
short-term investors took as a signal to scoop up raw materials.
But the dollar has abruptly reversed course in the past two days, battering commodities markets in the latest
show of volatility that has kept investors across financial markets on edge in recent weeks. Many investors
had been skeptical the commodities rally would last, arguing that supplies were robust and demand was still
soft amid economic weakness in Europe and Asia.
"We have come to a pause in the commodities rally," said George Gero, a senior vice president with RBC
Capital Markets Global Futures. "Some people are taking the opportunity now to lock in profits."
The dollar is up 2.7% against the euro since Friday, as money managers seized on statements indicating the
European Central Bank will ramp up stimulus measures that likely would weigh on the common currency.
Adding to the euro's weakness is uncertainty over whether debt-laden Greece will be able to stay in the
currency union.
A stronger dollar weighs on commodity prices, which are denominated in the U.S. currency and become more
expensive for buyers outside the U.S. when the dollar climbs. The opposite also tends to hold true, traders
say.
U.S. oil prices were among the hardest hit Tuesday, sliding 3.7% to $57.26 a barrel, a three-week low.
Front-month copper sank 2.3% to $2.8615 a pound, the lowest price since April 29. Gold shed 1.7% to
$1,206.70 a troy ounce, while corn fell 1.6% to $3.62 a bushel. Energy is the S&P GSCI index's largest
component.
Tuesday's selloff in crude "should prove to be the beginning of a major price decline," said energy-advisory
firm Ritterbusch & Associates in a note.
Analysts and traders have warned that the crude-oil market is still too oversupplied to sustain U.S. prices
above about $60 a barrel. U.S. production has held largely flat this year despite large spending cuts and a
pullback in drilling. If prices hold above $60, market watchers say, shale-oil producers will be able to maintain
current production levels or even raise output, adding to the global glut of crude.
As well, recent data from China have sparked concerns that demand from the world's largest commodity
buyer remains weak, despite a series of government measures aimed at boosting the economy. The average
price of new homes in 70 Chinese cities fell for an eighth month in a row, down 6.3% in April from a year
earlier, government data showed Monday. Many investors use the construction and sale of new homes as a
gauge of economic health and raw-material demand.
Traders had little reaction to a more positive economic report from the U.S. Tuesday morning showing home
building soared in April after a sluggish winter sales season.
Investors are keeping their eye on Wednesday's release of the minutes from the Federal Reserve's latest
monetary-policy meeting for indications on whether the latest data has swayed the central bank's opinions

--- Page 134 ---
Page 134 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
on when to raise benchmark interest rates. Higher rates make a currency more attractive for investors and
tend to boost its value.

The S&P 500 index eked out another record, capping a quiet week of trading.
Even as money continues to flow into U.S. stocks, traders said there is little enthusiasm among investors

million William Blair Small Cap Growth Fund. U.S. investors "have nowhere else to go," he said. Treasury
bonds offer relatively anemic yields and cash is a losing investment after taking inflation into account, he
said.

Jones Industrial Average rose 20.32 points, or 0.1%, to 18272.56, while the Nasdaq Composite Index slipped
2.50 points, or less than 0.1%, to 5048.29.

exchange-traded fund rose 1% Friday.

for lending profits, fell Friday. Financial shares in the S&P 500 fell 0.4%.

0.09% below its March 2 record close of 18288.63. Year to date, the S&P 500 is up 3.1%, while the Dow is up
2.5%.
Economic data have continued to damp enthusiasm among U.S. stock investors, traders say. Though this
past week's jobless-claims number was strong, April retail sales were weak.
Friday brought more lackluster data. Industrial production, which measures the output of U.S. manufacturers,
utilities and mines, decreased for the fifth consecutive month in April, the Federal Reserve said. The data
suggested weak global demand, a stronger dollar and lower oil prices continue to limit output.
And a preliminary reading on sentiment showed consumers turned more skeptical about the economy in early
May. The turn suggests there will be no quick pickup in consumer spending, investors said. "The data has
been OK, not as robust as people would have liked . . . and that's why markets are a little bit jumpier," said
Jack Caffrey, equity portfolio manager at J.P. Morgan Private Bank.
In commodity markets, gold futures rose just 10 cents Friday to $1,225.50 an ounce. Crude oil fell 0.3% to
$59.69 a barrel.

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Wealth Manager: Total Return: Highlights of the Journal's personal-finance blog
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Wealth Manager: Total Return: Highlights of the Journal's personal-finance
blog
912 words
16 May 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B9
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Pick the Right Plastic
To Use While Overseas
The strong dollar has made this an attractive summer for venturing abroad. Travelers can simplify their
finances -- and help their money go even further -- with a smart plan for how to pay for purchases and get
cash.
Plan to use plastic for most spending. Travelers generally get better currency-exchange rates when they use
credit or debit cards to make purchases rather than exchanging dollars for local currency.
Pick a suitable primary card. Many cards have a foreign-transaction fee, such as 2% or 3% of the transaction
amount. But there are plenty of cards -- particularly credit cards -- that don't have these fees. A bank-issued
card in the Visa or MasterCard network is likely to be accepted in more locations than an American Express
or Discover card, credit-card specialists say.
Have a Backup Card, Too. Among the reasons to bring at least two cards: A card could get lost or stolen, or a
spate of overseas charges could lead a card issuer to freeze your account.
Before You Go. Notify your bank and card issuers of your travel plans. Make sure you have phone numbers --
ideally toll-free numbers -- for these companies. And keep photocopies of the fronts and backs of your cards
with someone at home and with you, separate from the cards.
-- Karen Damato
Most People Get an 'F'
On 529-Plan Knowledge
Despite being around for more than 15 years, 529 college-savings plans remain a mystery to most
households.
Sixty-six percent of Americans don't know what a 529 plan is, according to findings released Wednesday by
financial-services firm Edward Jones. The firm's survey asked just over 1,000 U.S. adults -- including parents
and those without children -- whether a 529 plan is a college-savings plan, a retirement plan, a type of life
insurance or a low-cost health-care plan.
Forty-one percent answered incorrectly, with retirement plan as the most commonly selected response, and
25% said they didn't know.
With 529 plans, earnings grow tax-deferred and can be withdrawn tax-free for qualified higher-education
expenses, such as tuition and room and board.
A recent report from lender SLM Corp., or Sallie Mae, found that among parents saving for college, 27% are
using 529s.
-- AnnaMaria Andriotis
Is a Co-Branded Credit
Card for You?

--- Page 137 ---
Page 137 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Merchants and financial institutions are expressing renewed enthusiasm for co-branded credit cards, which
can be used practically anywhere while also promoting a particular company or retailer. For customers, the
cards can be a great way to rack up perks and points with a favorite merchant.
The key -- as with all credit cards -- is to find one that suits your spending habits and lifestyle.
Annual fees. Don't reject them out of hand. Paying an annual fee might be worthwhile if the fee can easily
and quickly be offset by perks. For example, the United MileagePlus Explorer card from J.P. Morgan Chase
costs $95 a year, but includes a free checked bag for the cardholder and a companion on the same United
Airlines reservation.
Interest rates. If you don't pay off your credit-card bill each month, it is wise to compare interest rates on
co-branded cards against traditional cards. A survey last year by CreditCards.com found that 27 co-branded
cards had an average rate of 21.63%. The average fixed credit-card interest rate is 13.02%, according to
Bankrate.com.
Fine print. Co-branded cards are known for wooing customers with big sign-up perks. But in many cases, the
customers don't get the perk until they spend a set amount over a certain time. Travelers who sign up for the
Gold Delta SkyMiles card from American Express get 30,000 bonus miles, but only after making $1,000 in
purchases on the card within the first three months.
Smaller, but valuable perks. Some smaller perks can be a sweet deal, particularly if there isn't an annual fee.
The LoveLoft card, issued by Comenity Bank, gives customers $15 off on an in-store Loft purchase during
their birthday month and offers a 10% discount on the first Tuesday of every month.
-- Robin Sidel
One in Four Workers
Loses Out on Free Money
About one quarter of Americans fail to pocket the full matching contribution offered in workplace 401(k)
retirement plans, according to a study published Tuesday by Financial Engines, a Sunnyvale, Calif., firm that
manages 401(k) accounts for individual participants.
On average, employees who fall short leave $1,336 -- or 2.4% of their salaries -- on the table annually, said
the firm, which examined the records of 4.4 million retirement-plan participants at 553 companies. That
represented an average 60% of the matching funds available.
Nationwide, Financial Engines estimates that 401(k) participants are passing up $24 billion in such funds
annually. "Employer matching contributions play an important role in making the 401(k) a viable retirement
vehicle for Americans by . . . adding a significant subsidy to personal savings," says the report.
Currently, the most common matching formula provides $1 for every $1 an employee saves, up to 6% of pay,
according to Aon Hewitt, a 401(k) administrator.
-- Anne Tergesen
