Stocks Pull Back After Wednesday's Surge
By Dan Strumpf
614 words
20 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks fell, pulling back a day after the Federal Reserve spurred a sharp rally by striking a cautious note
about the U.S. economy.
The slide wiped out roughly half of Wednesday's gains, as investors reassessed views on the Fed's
reluctance in increasing borrowing costs. Other markets that advanced on Wednesday also reversed course,
from the euro to bonds and crude oil. Though the central bank stressed it will take a slow approach to rate
increases, higher short-term benchmark rates still are widely anticipated later this year.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average dropped 117.16 points, or 0.7%, to 17959.03, while the S&P 500 index
lost 10.39, or 0.5%, to 2089.11.
Shares of energy companies posted some of the sharpest losses as oil prices retreated, with the S&P 500
Energy Index shedding 1.7%.
Other market barometers posted gains. The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 9.55, or 0.2%, to 4992.38. The
Russell 2000 index, which tracks shares of small-capitalization companies, rose 2.72, or 0.2%, to 1254.86 --
its second record high in a row.
In its statement on monetary policy Wednesday, the Fed dropped its promise to remain "patient" before
lifting interest rates, a widely expected shift that opened the door to rate increases this year. But downbeat
economic forecasts and comments by Fed Chairwoman Janet Yellen indicated the central bank remains in
no hurry to raise rates. Those remarks caught many investors off guard and sent shares rallying. But then the
euphoria died down.
"I just think it's a little bit of a breather, post-that-somewhat-surprising move" on Wednesday, said Brian
Fenske, head of sales trading at ITG.
On Thursday, the dollar resumed its climb, with the euro falling 1.9% to $1.0661.
Shares of Apple fell 97 cents, or 0.8%, to $127.50 on its first day of trading as a Dow component. Shares of
AT&T, replaced by Apple in the Dow, fell 39 cents, or 1.2%, to 33.20.
After a lull in February, stocks are once again encountering the kind of big swings that marked the start of
2015. Investors are wrestling with the Fed's shifting monetary policy and the impact of the strengthening
dollar on corporate earnings. The Dow is down 1% this month and is up 0.8% for the year.
Many investors say the Fed's caution on interest rates bodes well for stocks. Michael Ball, portfolio manager
at Weatherstone Capital Management, which oversees $785 million, said the Fed's actions on Wednesday
have helped ease fears of an imminent rate rise.
"I think that Janet Yellen played it masterfully," he said. "They've given themselves more wiggle room than
they have in the past."
Mr. Ball said he recently cashed out of his bets in energy stocks amid a steep slide that has sent the
benchmark U.S. oil price down 59% since late June. He boosted his weighting in transportation and
technology shares instead.
European markets were mixed. Germany's DAX slipped 0.2% while France's CAC-40 gained 0.1%.
In commodity markets, crude-oil futures declined 1.6% to $43.96 a barrel. Gold futures gained 1.5% to
$1169.10 an ounce.

Shares of Target fell 46 cents, or 0.6%, to 80.60 after announcing plans to boost the pay of all its workers to

Investors Celebrate Gentler Tone From Officials
Wednesday's Markets
Investors Celebrate Gentler Tone From Officials
By Min Zeng and Corrie Driebusch
789 words
19 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
For weeks, investors have been focused on a single word: "patient." But it was the combination of many
others that grabbed their attention Wednesday and gave financial markets one of the biggest jolts in years.
As expected, the Federal Reserve removed the word "patient" from its statement on plans for interest-rate
rises, giving the central bank the green light to start considering increases as early as June.
But the missive included many downbeat phrases about the state of the U.S. economy, suddenly raising the
possibility that any move may come later in the year than many previously expected.
The comments wrong-footed some investors and caused sharp reversals across markets. The dollar, which
had been on a monthslong tear against the euro, saw its biggest drop against the common currency in six
years. Treasury yields, which had been edging higher in anticipation of rising rates, plummeted.
"The Fed caught everyone offside," said Tom di Galoma, head of rates and credit trading at brokerage firm
ED&F Man Capital Markets. Wednesday's trading "was completely unreal, as everyone was looking to buy at
once,"
Stock prices rose. The Dow Jones Industrial Average gained almost 200 points in the minutes following the
Fed's release of its postmeeting statement and then closed up 227.11 points, or 1.3%, at 18076.19.
The S&P 500 rose 1.2% to 2099.50.
The Nasdaq briefly moved above 5000, before falling back. It ended the day 45.39 points higher, or 0.9%, at
4982.83. The Russell 2000 index of small-company stocks hit a record, climbing 9.9 points, or 0.8%, to
1252.14.
Overseas early Thursday, Australia's S&PASX 200 was up 1.5% and Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index was up
0.7%, but Japan's Nikkei Stock Average was down 0.9%.
Investors now see little chance of the Fed raising rates in June. Fed-funds futures, used to place bets on
central-bank policy, show investors and traders seeing a 23% likelihood of a rate increase in June,
compared with 40% before the Fed statement, according to data from Citigroup Global Markets.
Odds for an increase at the September Fed meeting came down as well, to 80%, from 111% before the
statement.
"The Fed dropped the word 'patient,' but they remain patient in raising rates," said Mark MacQueen,
co-founder and portfolio manager at Sage Advisory Services Ltd., which oversees $11 billion in assets in
Austin, Texas. "A rate increase in June is off the table, and the bond market is rallying."
That shift led the yield on the two-year note, which is especially sensitive to changes in the Fed's policy
outlook, to drop the most on a one-day basis since December 2010. Yields fall as bond prices rise.
The yield on the two-year note plunged to 0.560% from 0.669% Tuesday.
Investors also piled into longer-term U.S. government bonds, sending the benchmark 10-year note's yield
below 2%. It finished the day at 1.945%.
"It was a grabfest in the bond market," said Larry Milstein, a debt trader at R.W. Pressprich & Co. in New
York.
The dollar, meanwhile, tumbled toward its biggest one-day decline against the euro since March 2009.

--- Page 186 ---
Page 186 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The move marked a sharp reversal of the dollar's recent rally, which came as investors saw the Fed raising
rates at a time when the European Central Bank was embarking on a new round of aggressive economic
stimulus.
The euro rose 2.5% to $1.0864. The greenback has strengthened 27% against the euro in the past year, and
some analysts had been calling for the two currencies to hit parity.
"Perhaps this takes a near-term push to parity with the euro off the table," said Joe Manimbo, senior market
analyst at Western Union. "Right now, it certainly is a good excuse to pare back" bullish bets on the dollar.
The dollar dropped against the Japanese currency, to 120.11 yen, down 1% for the day.
The weakening dollar helped U.S. oil prices snap a six-session losing streak.
Light, sweet crude for April delivery settled up $1.20, or 2.8%, at $44.66 a barrel on the New York Mercantile
Exchange, after falling to a six-year intraday low earlier in the day, following the release of government data
showing crude inventories in the U.S. at a record. Before the Fed announcement, oil had been hovering
above $42 a barrel.
Gold for March delivery, the front-month contract, added $3.10, or 0.3%, to $1,151.40 an ounce.

Blue Chips Drop, But Nasdaq Gains
Tuesday's Markets
Blue Chips Drop, But Nasdaq Gains
By Dan Strumpf
611 words
18 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks fell as the Federal Reserve kicked off a two-day policy meeting where investors expect the
central bank to signal that higher interest rates are on the horizon.
The Fed is widely expected to remove its promise to be "patient" before raising interest rates from its
postmeeting policy statement, due Wednesday. That shift is seen by investors as paving the way for the Fed
to raise interest rates in gradual fashion sometime later this year.
Ahead of the results of the Fed meeting, trading was quiet. The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 128.34
points, or 0.7%, to 17849.08, while the S&P 500 lost 6.91, or 0.3%, to 2074.28.
Overseas early Wednesday, stocks in Shanghai climbed to near a seven-year high, as hopes for further
stimulus from Beijing encouraged investors. The Shanghai Composite Index was up 0.8% to 3528.71, its
highest since May 2008. Elsewhere, stocks were down in Japan, Australia, the Philippines and Indonesia.
"Everyone's waiting for the Fed," said Gordon Charlop, managing director at Rosenblatt Securities.
Technology stocks bucked the broader market, with the tech-heavy Nasdaq Composite gaining 7.93 points,
or 0.2%, to 4937.43.
Investors have credited near-zero interest rates for boosting the appeal of stocks and helping to propel the
market's six-year bull run.
With higher rates looming, stock benchmarks have seen wider swings this month as investors grapple with
expectations about the central bank's course.
Also buffeting shares has been the stronger U.S. dollar, which investors expect will hurt profits at U.S.-based
multinational companies.
The S&P 500 is down 1.4% this month but remains just 2% from its March 2 record. On Tuesday, materials
stocks posted the heaviest losses among S&P 500 companies, falling 1.2%. Shares of energy companies fell
0.5% as oil prices plumbed new six-year lows.
"We're entering a more recent trend of bigger daily swings," said Larry Weiss, head of trading at Instinet.
"There's a lot of currency moves, a lot of speculation about rate increases in the U.S."
Michael Scanlon, senior investment analyst on the $1.5 billion John Hancock Balanced fund, said the fund
dialed back its stockholdings late last year in expectation of greater volatility in 2015. He said he expects the
Fed to raise rates in the fourth quarter or early next year, but said stocks likely will post stronger gains as the
economy picks up in the second half of 2015.
"We expected a pretty bumpy first half of the year," Mr. Scanlon said. "I still think equities are the place to be
and the U.S. is the place to be."
In other markets, the dollar weakened 0.3% against the euro, pulling back from its recent highs. The
eurozone currency rose to $1.0598, from $1.0570 Monday. European stocks fell. France's CAC-40 declined
0.6% and Germany's DAX sank 1.5%.
U.S.-traded crude-oil futures fell 1% to $43.46 a barrel, its lowest settlement since March 2009. Gold for
March delivery fell 0.4% to $1,148.30 an ounce. Treasury prices rose, pushing the 10-year yield down to
2.059%, from 2.098% on Monday.

Higher-Rate Expectations Tarnish Gold --- The precious metal falls to four-month low as the strong dollar cuts investors' appetite for it
Commodities Report
Higher-Rate Expectations Tarnish Gold --- The precious metal falls to four-month low as the strong
dollar cuts investors' appetite for it
By Tatyana Shumsky
769 words
18 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold sank to a four-month low on Tuesday, succumbing to expectations for higher interest rates in the U.S.
Many investors are anticipating that the Federal Reserve's policy-making committee will signal an increased
readiness to raise the benchmark short-term rate in a statement due Wednesday.
That is likely to put even more downward pressure on gold, these investors say, largely due to the dollar's
influence on the price of the precious metal. The prospect of higher rates -- and bigger returns than those
found elsewhere -- has attracted money to U.S. stocks and bonds, boosting the dollar's value against major
currencies such as the euro and the yen. Exacerbating the greenback's climb are central banks in Japan and
Europe, which continue to pursue easy-money policies.
A stronger dollar makes gold relatively more expensive for buyers outside the U.S., creating a headwind for
demand. Investors' waning appetite for gold illustrates the ripple effects that the recent sharp dollar rally has
had across financial markets.
The most actively traded contract, for April delivery, fell $5, or 0.4%, to end at $1,148.20 a troy ounce on the
Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. That is the lowest close since Nov. 6, when the futures
price slid to a four-year low of $1,142.60 a troy ounce.
Many market watchers expect the dollar to benefit from any changes to the wording in the Fed statement that
would indicate central-bank officials are moving closer to raising rates. In particular, investors are looking to
see if the Fed will drop the phrase that it will be "patient in beginning to normalize its stance on monetary
policy." Fed officials in recent weeks have expressed confidence in the recovery of the labor market, which
Fed Chairwoman Janet Yellen herself has said is key to any rate decision.
"In this environment, where the U.S. dollar is strong and will probably get stronger with a rise in rates, that
won't be good for gold," said Daniel Belchers, a commodities-fund manager at Threadneedle Investments,
which has $600 million invested in commodities. Mr. Belchers said his fund is underweight gold versus its
benchmark. "If you're looking at U.S. dollar strength, and U.S. economic strength, it's going to be difficult for
gold to do anything in that environment," he said.
As a group, money-manager bets on higher gold prices fell to a four-month low of 48,673 bullish futures and
options contracts in the week ended March 10, according to weekly data from the U.S. Commodity Futures
Trading Commission.
There also are other factors at play. Fund managers see less need for haven investments amid political
stability in Greece and a move toward peace in Ukraine. Inflation has remained subdued, giving investors
less reason to hold gold as a hedge against rising prices. And relatively steady economic growth in the U.S.
has kept stocks lofty, dimming gold's allure.
The dollar has marched to a 12-year high against the euro and a seven-year high against the yen amid rising
expectations for a U.S. rate increase later this year.
Odds of an increase happening at the Fed's July meeting rose to 42% on Tuesday, from 38.4% a month ago,
according to federal-funds futures data from CME Group. Meanwhile, expectations for a September increase
rose to 61%, from 54.9% a month earlier.
To be sure, some investors are holding on to their gold in the belief that the dollar's rally is likely to sputter
once the Fed raises rates. Rick Rule, chairman of Sprott U.S. Holdings, said he advised his clients to hold as
much as 10% of their assets in gold.

--- Page 190 ---
Page 190 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Rule says the dollar could falter, bolstering gold, once investors realize that the Fed is unlikely to raise
rates rapidly after the first increase.
"With gold already in a bull market against almost every other currency other than the dollar, a declining
dollar would benefit the gold price," he said. Sprott U.S. Holdings, a part of Sprott Asset Management,
oversees $400 million invested mainly in mining stocks and metals.
Other investors are taking a wait-and-see approach. Manne Rasmussen, who helps manage $600 million at
Vontobel Belvista Commodity Fund in Zurich, said his fund has been closing out bets on lower gold prices
ahead of the Fed decision.

Stocks Rebound as Dollar Weakens --- Investors are focusing on the currency's moves and Fed signals on rates at this week's meeting
Monday's Markets
Stocks Rebound as Dollar Weakens --- Investors are focusing on the currency's moves and Fed
signals on rates at this week's meeting
By Saumya Vaishampayan and Jon Hilsenrath
874 words
17 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks and Treasurys rose and the dollar's rally took a breather, as investors looked ahead to the
Federal Reserve's policy meeting this week against a backdrop of softer economic news.
Fed officials are widely expected to alter the guidance they give the public about interest rates Wednesday,
dropping an assurance that they will be "patient" in deciding when to start raising rates.
The change will formally open the door to a possible rate increase in June, though Fed officials aren't yet set
on acting then. A number of downbeat reports in recent days suggest the U.S. economy may be reverting to
sluggishness after a burst of stronger growth last summer, raising new questions about whether 2015 will be
a breakout year.
Moreover, low inflation -- reinforced by a strong dollar -- could give Fed officials pause about rate increases.
Expectations of a Fed rate increase have in recent weeks capped stocks' rally, sent bond yields higher and
fueled a surge in the U.S. dollar against the euro to a 12-year high. But Monday brought a reversal of some of
those trends, especially in stocks, which have seen back-and-forth trading since the Dow Jones Industrial
Average and S&P 500 hit their most recent records on March 2.
The Dow advanced 228.11 points Monday, or 1.3%, to 17977.42. The S&P 500 gained 27.79 points, or 1.4%,
to 2081.19.
As has been the case in recent weeks, much of the focus for stock investors was on the dollar Monday. The
dollar's rally took a breather as the euro rose 0.7% to $1.0570. Traders said that move was enough to give
U.S. stocks a bounce.
Overseas early Tuesday, stocks also rose. Australia's benchmark S&PASX 200 was up 1.1%, Japan's Nikkei
Stock Average was up 0.8%, while Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index was up 0.3%.
"The dollar has weakened a little bit, and that's got people somewhat more excited" about U.S. stocks, said
Ian Winer, head of equities trading at Wedbush Securities.
Investors have grown increasingly concerned that the rising dollar will hurt the earnings of the U.S.
multinational companies that dominate the S&P 500. A stronger currency makes exports of U.S.-produced
goods more expensive compared to those produced overseas and reduces the value of profits earned in
foreign currencies.
The U.S. currency has risen about 14% against the euro this year as the European Central Bank embarked
on a new round of monetary-policy easing at the same time as investors expect the Fed to begin raising
interest rates later this year.
Fed Chairwoman Janet Yellen said in late February that a removal of the "patient" wording doesn't mean an
imminent rate increase will follow and that the timing depends on how the economy performs.
On Monday, the Fed reported that manufacturing output fell for the third consecutive month, indicating that
the surging dollar and global economic woes are weighing on factories.
Meanwhile, a measure of home-builder confidence declined for the third month in a row in March, the
National Association of Home Builders said on Monday.
Earlier reports have shown falling retail sales in recent months and a big decline in home sales in January --
defying expectations that Americans would step up spending amid the monthslong drop in gasoline prices.

--- Page 192 ---
Page 192 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"It's not weak enough that the Fed is likely to backpedal," said Gina Martin Adams, equity strategist at Wells
Fargo Securities. The Fed also could suggest that recent economic weakness has been related to the
weather, she said. We've had "a really nasty winter, which is naturally going to hold back activity," she added.
With help from the weaker-than-expected economic reports, U.S. government bonds strengthened on
Monday. In late-afternoon trading, the yield on the benchmark 10-year Treasury note was 2.098%, down from
2.110% on Friday. As bond prices rise, their yields fall.
"The bond market is saying that the Fed is not going to tighten imminently," said Mary Ann Hurley, vice
president of trading in Seattle at D.A. Davidson & Co. "Everyone expects 'patient' to be gone but that the Fed
will [raise interest rates] in slow and measured steps."
In European stock markets, France's CAC-40 gained 1% and Germany's DAX rallied 2.2% for its 26th record
of the year.
In commodity markets, crude-oil futures fell 2.1% to $43.88. That marked the fifth session in a row of
declines. Gold futures rose 0.1% to $1,153.30 an ounce.
In corporate news, Valeant Pharmaceuticals International raised its takeover price for Salix Pharmaceuticals
to $173 a share, or about $11.1 billion in total. Endo International subsequently withdrew its rival bid. Valeant
shares rose $4.91, or 2.5%, to $202.34. Salix shares added 3.35, or 2%, to 172.75, and shares of Endo
gained 2.32, or 2.7%, to 89.65.

Stocks Hurt by Strong Dollar --- Analysts have been downgrading estimates for corporate earnings as the currency gains
Friday's Markets
Stocks Hurt by Strong Dollar --- Analysts have been downgrading estimates for corporate earnings as
the currency gains
By Dan Strumpf and Corrie Driebusch
871 words
14 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks fell, as a rising dollar sparked fresh worries about corporate earnings.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average declined 145.91 points, or 0.8%, to 17749.31 on Friday. The S&P 500
index fell 12.55, or 0.6%, to 2053.40, while the Nasdaq Composite shed 21.53, or 0.4%, to 4871.76.
The slide marks the third consecutive week of losses for both the Dow and the S&P, which are both down for
the year. The Dow shed 0.6% during the past week, while the S&P 500 lost 0.9%.
Stocks fell from the opening bell Friday, with the Dow down as much as 265 points around midday. Shares
recovered ground during the final hour of trading, but neither the Dow nor the S&P entered positive territory
all day.
Traders pointed to the rising dollar as driving the bulk of market action. On Friday, the euro fell to a 12-year
low against the dollar, trading down 1.3% to $1.0497 following a slight rebound on Thursday that coincided
with stock-market gains.
A stronger dollar hurts the profit outlook for U.S.-based multinational companies in part by making their goods
more expensive and thus less competitive overseas. At the same time, as foreign currencies fall against the
dollar, the value of profits earned abroad is reduced.
"This whole move is being driven by one thing and one thing only, and that's the dollar," said Rick Fier,
director of execution services at Conifer Securities. "It's going to crush earnings. You're not going to get any
sort of outlook that's any good."
Shares of small companies have fared better amid the dollar rally. Small firms tend to be more U.S.-focused
and less exposed to currency fluctuations. The Russell 2000 index of small-capitalization companies fell 0.4%
Friday and rose 1.2% for the week.
"The dollar has been one of the biggest surprises in terms of how it continues to strengthen," said David
Lefkowitz, senior equity strategist at UBS Wealth Management Research.
Stocks have swerved higher and lower during this past week as investors grapple with the strong dollar and
look for clues on the timing and pace of the Federal Reserve's interest-rate increases and the implications for
the market.
In the coming week, the Fed is scheduled to hold a two-day policy meeting that could shed further light on the
central bank's plans. Investors will be looking for signs that the Fed is laying the groundwork for an increase
in interest rates later this year.
The dollar's strength also has come as the European Central Bank began its latest round of easing efforts.
At the same time, analysts have been downgrading their estimates for corporate earnings in the quarters
ahead, due in large part to the stronger dollar. Profits among S&P 500 companies are expected to fall 4.9% in
the first quarter, down from an expected pace of growth of 3.8% at the end of last year, according to FactSet.
Still, traders said the recent declines haven't been met with large stock-market volume. On Friday, 6.7 billion
shares changed hands, below the average level for the year.
Traders say the market's stumble has been mostly driven by short-term investors rather than big, long-term
investors, many of whom remain bullish on stocks this year.

--- Page 194 ---
Page 194 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Angel Mata, head of institutional equity trading at Stifel Financial Corp., said he expects stocks to remain
volatile until investors get more clarity from the Fed on the timing and pace of rate increases.
"Market participants are going to applaud it once it gets here, but until it gets here it's going to create some
uneasiness," he said. "I think everybody has pretty much resigned themselves to a volatile market going
forward."
European stocks mostly advanced, with France's CAC-40 closing 0.5% higher and Germany's DAX rising
0.9%.
In commodities markets, crude-oil futures tumbled 4.7% to $44.84 a barrel after the International Energy
Agency said U.S. production continues to climb despite low prices. Crude prices are down 58% since last

Gold futures edged higher by less than 0.1% to $1,152.60 an ounce.
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note gained 0.016 percentage point to 2.110% on Friday, as prices
declined. But for the week, the yield declined 0.129 percentage point.
Ann Inc. posted a surprise profit in its holiday quarter on stronger-than-expected sales growth, as the
company also outlined an effort to deliver an additional $35 million in cost savings by 2016. Shares of the
retailer climbed $3.02, or 8.1%, to $40.45.
Intel on Thursday lowered its revenue outlook for the first quarter by nearly $1 billion, a sign that demand for
personal computers is weakening amid sharp currency fluctuations and other factors. After falling 4.7%
Thursday, the shares Friday rose 13 cents, or 0.4%, to 30.93.

REVIEW --- Mind & Matter -- Fashion Chimps: Grass in Your Ear Is So You, Dear
REVIEW --- Mind & Matter -- Fashion Chimps: Grass in Your Ear Is So You, Dear
By Robert M. Sapolsky
695 words
14 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
We expect words such as "culture" and "technology" to appear in history textbooks or sociology articles, in
newspapers or Silicon Valley prospectuses. But those words also appear regularly in animal-behavior
journals -- and, increasingly, in ways that would be familiar to humans who read fashion magazines and
follow fads.
The "culture" concept got jump-started in primatology in the 1940s, with reports of an all-time primate genius,
a Japanese macaque monkey named Imo. Her troop lived near a beach frequented by tourists who fed them,
and she invented a technique for washing and salting potatoes by dipping them into seawater. Then, not
resting on her laurels, Imo had another breakthrough. Tourists would toss the monkeys grains of wheat that
would get mixed with sand. Imo figured out that if she threw a handful of the mixture into the water, the sand
would sink, and the wheat would float -- and she could then collect the wheat.
Where does "culture" fit into Imo's biography? Her techniques spread to friends and relatives, continuing in
the group for years. By the classic definition of social anthropology, this constituted cultural transmission.
Meanwhile, nonhuman primates burst onto the technology scene with Jane Goodall's observation in 1960
that Tanzanian chimps make tools -- sticks modified for fishing tasty termites out of mounds. Subsequent
research showed tool use among chimps across Africa: rock hammers and anvils for cracking nuts,
clumped-up leaves for sopping up water from tree hollows, sticks sharpened for hunting.
Different populations make different tools. New techniques spread across social networks (i.e., among
chimps who hang out with each other). Youngsters pick up techniques by watching their mothers (with
daughters learning faster than sons, as the latter mess around and don't pay attention). Techniques spread
from one group to another when someone emigrates.
Strikingly, chimps display something else: conformity. Everyone does something the same way; then
someone stumbles on an equally effective alternative. That individual marches to a different drummer for a
while before dropping the invention and rejoining the technology herd.
So what's new in chimp-culture studies? The transmission of "arbitrary" behaviors. If there are two ways of
doing something, one, arbitrarily favored by an influential group member, becomes the norm. Or teach a
chimp an arbitrary sequence of actions and reward the chimp for them, and the behavior spreads.
Reporting recently in the journal Animal Cognition, Edwin van Leeuwen of the Max Planck Institute for
Psycholinguistics in the Netherlands and colleagues saw something irresistibly nutty in a 12-chimp group in
Zambia. Back in 2007, a female named Julie got it into her head to start walking around with a long, strawlike
blade of grass in her ear.
The behavior had no discernible function, and no other chimp had ever been observed to do the
"grass-in-ear-behavior" (what the authors call GIEB). Could she be trying to ease the irritation of a parasite or
infectious disease? The researchers ruled that out, because neighboring chimp groups weren't suffering from
ear problems, making it unlikely that Julie was afflicted.
Julie just liked having a piece of grass sticking out of her ear. So sue her.
The behavior caught on. Chimps soon went about their business with grass stuck in their ears. Julie remained
the queen of GIEB-ing. During the five-year study period, the other group members GIEB-ed from one to 36
times, whereas Julie clocked in with an untouchable 168 times. Eventually, two-thirds of Julie's group
GIEB-ed. The behavior continued even after her death in 2012 -- the gold standard of cultural transmission.
What is this about? Again, as far as our era's finest minds can tell, GIEB-ing is good for nothing. It doesn't get
you food or defend you from predators. It's a fad, like the human version. Individuals invent spiky blue hair or

--- Page 196 ---
Page 196 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
embed a permanent ink pattern in their skin or hang hoops from their ears (or from their noses, in another
culture). Everyone decides that looks cool. And starts GIEB-ing.

Stocks Bounce Back Sharply --- Weak retail sales are viewed as good news by investors nervous about Fed moves
Thursday's Markets
Stocks Bounce Back Sharply --- Weak retail sales are viewed as good news by investors nervous
about Fed moves
By Corrie Driebusch And Saumya Vaishampayan
735 words
13 March 2015
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2015 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
U.S. stocks rose, rebounding after the biggest two-day decline since late January.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average advanced 259.83 points, or 1.5%, to 17895.22. The S&P 500 index rose
25.71 points, or 1.3%, to 2065.95, and the Nasdaq Composite gained 43.35 points, or 0.9%, to 4893.29. For
the Dow and S&P 500, Thursday's rally was the largest since Feb. 3.
Overseas early Friday, the Nikkei Stock Average was up 1.4%, Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index was up 0.4%,
but Australia's S&P Index was up 0.4%, but Australia's ASX 200 was down 0.6%.
Thursday in the U.S. marked the latest in a string of back-and-forth sessions since the Dow and S&P 500
both hit their latest record highs on March 2. On Tuesday, the index fell more than 300 points, but with
Thursday's increase the Dow is up slightly for the week.
Stocks have been treading water as investors again question the impact that a Federal Reserve rate increase
would have on the market. In the wake of a stronger-than-expected employment report for February, traders
said the stock market has lapsed back into a "good news is bad news" mode when it comes to the economy.
In that light, positive economic reports are seen by some investors as a negative for stocks because they
could prompt the Fed to raise rates as soon as June, removing an important support for market. Weak
economic news, by contrast, is seen as giving the Fed room to hold off longer on a rate rise.
Against that backdrop, investors attributed Thursday's rally in part to a weaker-than-forecast retail sales
report.
Retail sales fell 0.6% in February, marking the third month in a row of declining sales, the Commerce
Department said Thursday. Economists surveyed by The Wall Street Journal expected total sales to rise
0.2% last month.
"Retail sales numbers were much weaker than anyone expected, so that gives the Fed more leeway to hold
off before raising rates, and that's why the market is rallying," said Larry Swedroe, director of research for
BAM Alliance, a network of wealth managers that oversees about $25 billion.
Still, Mr. Swedroe said he is skeptical of giving the numbers too much weight, and isn't recommending any
allocation shifts because of the data.
"Focusing on news like this is like going uphill with a yo-yo and focusing on the yo-yo going up and down
instead of where you're going," he said. "It's a good way to get injured."
The only sector in the red for the day was energy, with energy companies in the S&P 500 falling 0.5%. The
shares fell along with the price of crude oil, which declined 2.3% to $47.05 a barrel.
For months, analysts have been predicting that lower oil and gas prices will boost consumer spending. Oil
prices have tumbled more than 50% since last June. Increased consumer spending should in turn boost
corporate profits, helping push stocks higher.
But the pickup in spending hasn't yet materialized, even as the economy continues to add jobs.
"With more people employed and lower oil prices . . . you'll start to see the underlying strength of American
consumers," said Ben Pace, chief investment officer of HPM Partners.

--- Page 198 ---
Page 198 of 198 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Treasury prices rose and the dollar fell. The yield on the 10-year note slipped to 2.094%, from 2.110%
Wednesday. The euro rose 0.8% to $1.0637.
Gold futures rose 0.1% to $1,152.10 an ounce.
In European stock markets, Germany's DAX slipped 0.1% after hitting its 24th all-time high for the year
Wednesday. France's CAC-40 fell 0.2%.
In corporate news, Dow component United Technologies said it would explore strategic alternatives for its
Sikorsky Aircraft business, which is known for its Black Hawk helicopters. Its shares rose $2.90, or 2.5%, to
$121.24.
Walt Disney said at its annual shareholders meeting that it plans to produce a sequel to its hit animated
movie "Frozen." It shares rose $4.28, or 4.2%, to $107.17.
